    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4dfd57fb24164597948c4991c2da33a7">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3b024fcb-fa65-458b-83ee-221dcf1a6b9f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <h1 class="citation__title">Enabling One-Step De-Sodiation of Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> Cathode via Regulating Coordination Environment for High-Power and Long-Lasting Sodium-Ion Batteries</h1>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d19537810" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NMVP) is considered as a promising cathode candidate for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) because it possesses a higher voltage plateau of 3.6 V (Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup>) besides the voltage plateau of 3.4 V (V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>), lower cost, and environmental benign compared to Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. However, such cathode still suffers from sluggish intrinsic Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and the Jahn-Teller distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup>, leading to low capacity and poor cycling performance. Particularly, the second-step Na<sup>+</sup> de-sodiation in NMVP is the rate-determining step owing to a lower chemical diffusion coefficient with one order of magnitude than that of the first-step counterpart. To address these issues, a coordination environment regulation strategy is reported to develop a one-step de-sodiation NMVP cathode via introducing Zr<sup>4+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup>/Ca<sup>2+</sup> into Mn and Na sites, respectively. Based on theoretical calculations and electrochemical evaluation, the obtained Na<sub>3.3</sub>K<sub>0.1</sub>Ca<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>VZr<sub>0.2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> exhibits much enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and efficiently inhibits the Jahn-Teller distortion. Importantly, such modification significantly facilitates the second-step Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion of NMVP, realizing one-step de-sodiation. When employed as a cathode for SIBs, such cathode shows a specific capacity of 73 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (15 C), and capacity retentions of 92.7% after 3000 cycles (at 10 C, room temperature), and 72.6% after 1000 cycles (1 C, 50&nbsp;°C).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202418642-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202418642-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received widespread attention due to the advantages of abundant and low cost sodium resources, and higher battery safety in contrast to lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0001_R_d19537802e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0005_R_d19537802e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, practical application of SIBs is still limited by suitable cathode and anode materials. Currently, it is highly imperative to develop high-performance and affordable cathode and anode materials for pushing commercial application of SIBs. At present, typical cathode materials mainly involve Prussian blue analogs, layered transition metal (TM) oxides, organic cathode materials, and polyanionic compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0006_R_d19537802e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0007_R_d19537802e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, sodium super ion conductor (NASICON)-type polyanionic compounds have attracted considerable interest owing to their open 3D ion diffusion channels, high redox potentials, and stable framework structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0006_R_d19537802e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0008_R_d19537802e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) is a typical NASICON-type polyanionic compound, which possesses a theoretical specific capacity of 117&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a redox potential of 3.4&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0008_R_d19537802e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0009_R_d19537802e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the relatively low working voltage (V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>: 3.4 V), high cost, and environmental issues of vanadium weaken its competitiveness in the area of large-scale energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0010_R_d19537802e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing a V-less or V-free polyanionic cathode would be a cost-competitive trend. In recent years, Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NMVP) has been demonstrated as a promising cathode material for SIBs because it not only possesses a higher working voltage (Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup>: 3.6 V) but also significantly reduces cost by 35% corresponding to NVP, which is very favorable for energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0010_R_d19537802e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NMVP usually exhibits two voltage platforms at 3.4 and 3.6 V at a voltage window of 2.5–3.8 V corresponding to a two-step de-sodiation reaction, which is assigned to the redox couples of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0010_R_d19537802e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett.">10</a>-<a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0012_R_d19537802e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Small">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>However, such cathode still suffers from some critical issues: i) sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of the second-step de-sodiation. The NMVP, with its Na(2) sites entirely occupied by Na ions, has a higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier than NVP with one Na(2) vacancy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0013_R_d19537802e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the rate-determining step, the chemical diffusion coefficients of the second-step de-sodiation (corresponding to the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup> oxidation reaction) were over one order of magnitude lower than that of the first-step de-sodiation (corresponding to the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> oxidation reaction), seriously restricting Na<sup>+</sup> mobility;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0014_R_d19537802e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0015_R_d19537802e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ii) low electronic conductivity. The VO<sub>6</sub>/MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, separated by PO<sub>4</sub>, fails to form a continuous path for efficient electron conduction, resulting in poor electronic conductivity. This issue is a common problem of almost all polyanionic cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0013_R_d19537802e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0014_R_d19537802e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> iii) unsatisfactory structural stability. The Jahn-Teller (JT) distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> induces structural changes, irreversible phase transitions and thus results in poor cyclability, especially at high temperatures and high rates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0010_R_d19537802e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett.">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0016_R_d19537802e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To address low electronic conductivity and the JT distortion, carbon coating and doping for NMVP cathode have been demonstrated as the most efficient strategies in which the former can improve surface charge transport while the latter can stabilize MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and crystal structure by introducing heteroatoms with robust TM─O bond at Mn sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0014_R_d19537802e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0017_R_d19537802e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a>-<a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0021_R_d19537802e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, doping can manipulate local coordination environment of TM and Na sites for reducing Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier. For example, Chen et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0022_R_d19537802e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Phys. Chem. C">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> found that doping K ions at the Na sites of NVP can increase operating voltage, suppress lattice changes during charging-discharging process, and accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Ding et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0013_R_d19537802e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> experimentally established that doping K<sup>+</sup> at the Na(1) site of NMVP effectively lowers Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier, strengthens structural stability, improves operating voltage. Lu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0023_R_d19537802e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Electrochim. Acta">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> also reported the doping of K<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions at Na sites to boost the cell volume of NVP to enhance sodium ion diffusion kinetics. Lavela et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0024_R_d19537802e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Power Sources.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated that Cr substitution at Mn sites resulted in an increased diffusion coefficient for sodium ions. Hu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0021_R_d19537802e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized Al-substituted Na<sub>4</sub>V<sub>0.8</sub>Al<sub>0.2</sub>Mn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode with superior Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and structural stability. The authors attributed the superior performance of the Na<sub>4</sub>V<sub>0.8</sub>Al<sub>0.2</sub>Mn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode to the strong Al─O bonding, which suppressed the JT distortion, enhanced ion-covalent character, and lessened total volume change during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion. Although the above carbon coating or doping strategies can efficiently improve sodium storage properties of NMVP, boosting the second-step de-sodiation reaction (rate-determining step) of Na<sup>+</sup> migration capability of NMVP to the same level as the first-step de-sodiation reaction has not been studied so far.</p>
         
         <p>NMVP cathode usually undergoes a two-step de-sodiation/sodiation reaction that refers to a single-phase solid solution reaction and a two-phase reaction at the voltage window of 2.5─3.8 V, respectively. The charge/discharge behavior is associated with the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions at Na(2) sites, whereas the Na<sup>+</sup> ions at Na(1) sites are generally electrochemically inert owing to stronger Na─O bond at Na(1) sites compared to Na(2) sites. The first-step Na<sup>+</sup> de-sodiation process is associated with the oxidation of V<sup>3+</sup>, in which Na<sup>+</sup> migrates in 3D channels with a relatively rapid ion diffusion rate. The second-step Na<sup>+</sup> de-sodiation process is related to the oxidation of Mn<sup>2+</sup>, during which Na<sup>+</sup> diffuses along the c-axis in the 1D channel, resulting in a slower diffusion of sodium ions. The main reason is that with the extraction of sodium ions, the Na(2) sites will preferentially form empty sites, and the filled Na(1) sites hinder the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions between the active Na(2) sites, which makes the ion diffusion coefficient of the second-step Na<sup>+</sup> extraction reaction more than one order of magnitude lower than that of the first-step process. Ohno et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0025_R_d19537802e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> revealed that both a and c axes of the NVP lattice undergo a slight contraction along with the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, which would be detrimental to sodium ion diffusion. Ma et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0014_R_d19537802e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> also further experimentally confirmed that ion diffusion kinetics in NMVP material is faster in single-phase solid solution reactions than in two-phase reactions. Therefore, improving the second-step kinetics of sodium extraction reaction in the NMVP cathode is especially important for enhancing rate capability and utilization of active material. Wu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0026_R_d19537802e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported Cl-doped Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPO<sub>2</sub>F), and discovered that Cl doping revised redox behaviors of V<sup>3+</sup> by altering electron distribution of dangling anions and V center. As demonstrated by the above cases, elemental doping can effectively modulate local electronic structure of active center atoms, thereby altering working voltage of the electrode and changing phase transition behavior of the electrode material during the charge and discharge process. The two redox couples in the NMVP cathode have a voltage difference of ≈ 200 mV (V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>: 3.4 V; Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup>: 3.6 V). Thus, it would be very favorable for practical application and also challenging for enabling the one-step realization of two V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup> redox couples in NMVP at the same potential by regulating local coordination environment of TM sites.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we achieve a one-step de-sodiaiton reaction of NMVP by synergistically regulating local coordination environments via introducing Zr<sup>4+</sup>, and K<sup>+</sup>/Ca<sup>2+</sup> at Mn sites and Na sites, respectively. In this work, we mainly focus on the effect of Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution on modulating electronic structure and Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics of NMVP since the optimizations of K<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> at Na sites have been demonstrated in our previous work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0013_R_d19537802e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0027_R_d19537802e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When evaluated as cathode for SIBs, the prepared Na<sub>3.3</sub>K<sub>0.1</sub>Ca<sub>0.1</sub>VMn<sub>0.8</sub>Zr<sub>0.2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NKCMVP-Zr0.2) exhibits a capacity retention rate of 92.7% after 3000 cycles at 10 C, which is superior to most of the NMVP cathode materials reported so far.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0020_R_d19537802e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0028_R_d19537802e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Lett.">28</a>-<a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0032_R_d19537802e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, such cathode also delivers a high capacity retention of 72.6% after 1000 cycles at 1 C at elevated temperature (50&nbsp;°C), ≈1.8 times higher than that of NMVP (40.0%) without Zr substitution. Such findings reveal that the introduction of Zr<sup>4+</sup> into Mn sites in NMVP can efficiently stabilize crystal structure and inhibit the JT distortion. More importantly, Zr substitution can alter sodium ion de-intercalation mechanism of NMVP, transforming the original two-step de-intercalation reaction into a one-step reaction. Based on the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) analysis, the chemical diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the second-step de-sodiation of Zr-substituted NMVP is ≈1.01&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is almost the same to the first-step de-sodiation reaction. The density functional theory (DFT) calculation was also employed to disclose the positive effect of Zr substitution on regulating local coordination environment of Mn sites for reducing band gap, inhibiting the JT distortion, and reducing Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier. This work offers a facile and efficient strategy to achieve one-step de-sodiation of NMVP cathode with significantly enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and structure stability for enabling high-rate and long-life SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202418642-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202418642-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The crystal structures of the as-prepared various NMVP cathode materials with Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution at Mn sites and K<sup>+</sup>/Ca<sup>2+</sup> substitution at Na sites (NKCMVP, NKCMVP-Zr0.05, NKCMVP-Zr0.1, NKCMVP-Zr0.2, NKCMVP-Zr0.3, and NKCMVP-Zr0.5) were analyzed using X-ray diffraction (XRD). In our previous work,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0013_R_d19537802e819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0027_R_d19537802e819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we have demonstrated the effect of Na-site regulation strategy on enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> migration of Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode by introducing K<sup>+</sup> or Ca<sup>2+</sup>. Compared to Na<sup>+</sup> ions (ionic radius:1.02 Å; electronegativity: 1.024), K<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> possess similar ionic radius (K<sup>+</sup>: 1.38 Å; Ca<sup>2+</sup>: 1.0 Å) and electronegativity values (K<sup>+</sup>: 0.998; Ca<sup>2+</sup>: 1.16), and prefer to occupy Na sites based on theoretical calculation and structural characterizations. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0001">1a</a>, the XRD patterns of all samples are assigned to the R-3c space group, and the sharp diffraction peaks indicate good crystallinity of all samples. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0001">1b</a> shows that the (116) peak gradually shifts to a lower angle with increasing Zr content, while the (211) and (300) peaks gradually shift to a higher angle, suggesting that Zr was successfully introduced into the crystal structure and led to an anisotropic change of the crystal lattice parameters. This may be attributed to dual effects of Zr<sup>4+</sup> ion substitution on the lattice parameters of NMVP. On the one hand, the large ionic radius (<i>r</i> = 0.72 Å) of Zr<sup>4+</sup> ions gives rise to the expansion of the cell volume in contrast to Mn<sup>2+</sup> (ionic radius: <i>r</i> = 0.67 Å), while on the other hand, the stronger Zr─O bond (bonding energy: ΔH<sub>Zr-O</sub> = 766.1±10.6 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) results in the contraction of the cell volume compared to Mn─O bonds (bonding energy: ΔH<sub>Mn-O</sub> = 362±25 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0020_R_d19537802e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0029_R_d19537802e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0033_R_d19537802e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the increasing Zr substitution content, the (104) peak gradually merges with the (110) peak and then splits again. Moreover, after the re-splitting, the intensity of the (110) peak remains higher than that of the (104) peak, which correlates with the gradual decrease of Mn elements and their gradual substitution by Zr elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0034_R_d19537802e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to high Zr doping, a small diffraction peak for NKCMVP-Zr0.3 appears at both ≈16° and ≈32.3°, suggesting that overhigh Zr content would generate the formation of a small amount of possible MnZr<sub>4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>6</sub> impurity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The generation of the impurity phase indicates the presence of a solid-solution limit for Zr doping. To probe the lattice parameter change of Zr substitution in NMVP, XRD Rietveld refinements were carried out for NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0001">1c,d</a>. Both the two samples show low R<sub>wp</sub>, indicating a high matching between the samples and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with the R-3c space group. Detailed crystallographic data obtained from the Rietveld refinements, including lattice parameters, atomic positions, and atomic occupancies are given in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S3</a> (Supporting Information). The structural model of the Zr-substituted sample was constructed using VESTA software, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0001">1f</a>. Compared to NKCMVP, NKCMVP-Zr0.2 exhibits a slight decrease in the a and b axes and an obvious increase in the c axis, with an overall increase in the lattice volume (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0001">1e</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which is attributed to larger ionic radius of Zr<sup>4+</sup> ions (<i>r</i> = 0.72 Å) than that of Mn<sup>2+</sup> (<i>r</i> = 0.67 Å). The increased cell volume would be beneficial for Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418642-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2220cc00-61fe-4bcd-b7d0-93eb82be7768/adfm202418642-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2220cc00-61fe-4bcd-b7d0-93eb82be7768/adfm202418642-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5022b076-8435-4fcd-9dec-a8477bab18ac/adfm202418642-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2220cc00-61fe-4bcd-b7d0-93eb82be7768/adfm202418642-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418642-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterizations of NKCMVP, NKCMVP-Zr0.05, NKCMVP-Zr0.1, NKCMVP-Zr0.2, NKCMVP-Zr0.3, and NKCMVP-Zr0.5. a) XRD patterns, the (104) diffraction peak identified by ♦, the ≈16° diffraction peak identified by ◊, and the ≈32.3° diffraction peak identified by ⁎; b) the partially enlarged XRD patterns corresponding to a); c) Rietveld refinement plots of NKCMVP; d) Rietveld refinement plots of NKCMVP-Zr0.2; d) lattice parameters of NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2; f) schematic crystal structure of NKCMVP-Zr0.2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The composition and surface valence states of the NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2 were investigated using XPS analysis. XPS survey spectra (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0002">2b</a>) demonstrated the elemental composition of the NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2, and it was found that, in addition to the Na, Mn, V, K, Ca, C, P, and O elements, Zr was successfully detected in the NKCMVP-Zr0.2, which, in combination with the above mentioned XRD tests, further proves that the Zr element was successfully doped into the NKCMVP. The V 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0002">2c</a>) demonstrates the valence state of V element. The binding energies of V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> are ≈517.0 eV and ≈525.5 eV, respectively, which are consistent with V<sup>3+</sup> reported in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0020_R_d19537802e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0035_R_d19537802e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The characteristic peaks in the Mn 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0002">2d</a>) that are at ≈642.0 and ≈653.7 eV correspond to Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, while the peaks at ≈646.5 eV, a distinct satellite peak of Mn<sup>2+</sup> was detected, indicating that the Mn ions in the samples existed in the form of +2 valence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0011_R_d19537802e1001" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Small Struct.">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0013_R_d19537802e1001" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0020_R_d19537802e1001" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The doping of Zr element had no significant effect on the valence states of V and Mn elements. The valence state of the Zr element was further analyzed and the characteristic peaks of Zr 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Zr 3d<sub>3/2</sub> located at ≈183.4 and ≈185.8 eV, respectively, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0002">2e</a> corresponding to Zr<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0020_R_d19537802e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0029_R_d19537802e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0033_R_d19537802e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The effect of Zr substitution on the valence states of V and Mn in the samples was further examined by soft-XAS. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0002">2g,h</a> displays the XAS spectra of V L<sub>2,3</sub> and Mn L<sub>2,3</sub>, respectively. The V L<sub>2,3</sub> spectra indicate that the Zr substitution has virtually no effect on the valence state of V, and the spectral line shapes of the multiplet spectral feature of the V element resemble that of the peaks of V<sup>3+</sup> as reported in the literature, which suggests that the valence state of V in the sample is mainly +3 valence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0019_R_d19537802e1033" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0030_R_d19537802e1033" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Storage Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Mn L<sub>2,3</sub> spectra indicate that Zr substitution has no influence on the valence state of Mn, and the spectral line shapes of Mn characteristic peaks are similar to those of Mn<sup>2+</sup> reported in the literature, suggesting that Mn exists in the sample at +2 valence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0019_R_d19537802e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0028_R_d19537802e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Lett.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result is consistent with that of XPS. In the XPS spectrum of C 1s (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0002">2f</a>), three different types of carbon were demonstrated, namely C─C, C─O, and O─C═O bonds. The high proportion of sp<sup>2</sup> C─C bonds indicates that the synthesized samples have excellent electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0021_R_d19537802e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0032_R_d19537802e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0002">2a</a>) further detected characteristic peaks of carbon, located at ≈1344 and ≈1592 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the G-band (sp<sup>2</sup> hybridization) and D-band (sp<sup>3</sup> hybridization) in carbon materials, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0036_R_d19537802e1068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The crystallinity of the carbon in the samples is usually reflected by the intensity ratio of the D and G bands.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0032_R_d19537802e1074" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value (0.877) for NKCMVP-Zr0.2 is less than that of NKCMVP (0.926), implying that the NKCMVP-Zr0.2 will have better electrical conductivity. NKCMVP-Zr0.2 shows a slightly lower I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value than that of NKCMVP, suggesting the slightly increased graphitization degree of carbon in the product. This is probably because the introduction of Zr<sup>4+</sup> in NKCMVP can induce the increase of sp<sup>2</sup>-type carbon. Based on the theoretical calculation findings (the detailed results are given in the latter discussion), Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution efficiently enhances intrinsic electronic conductivity of NKVMVP, which is likely to facilitate the formation of sp<sup>2</sup>-type carbon. The similar results have been also demonstrated by Ga or Co doping in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0037_R_d19537802e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Mater. Chem. A">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0038_R_d19537802e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Mater. Chem. A">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the TG curves show that NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2 possess the carbon content of 10.82% and 9.65%, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418642-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6a2c3cf0-afec-4b4b-8887-bb9e5e0d452f/adfm202418642-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6a2c3cf0-afec-4b4b-8887-bb9e5e0d452f/adfm202418642-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a29cde6c-551a-413e-9544-7f12b46c3539/adfm202418642-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6a2c3cf0-afec-4b4b-8887-bb9e5e0d452f/adfm202418642-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418642-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Raman spectra, b) XPS survey spectra, c) V 2p high-resolution XPS spectra, d) Mn 2p high-resolution XPS spectra, e) Zr 3d high-resolution XPS spectra, and f) C 1s high-resolution XPS spectra of NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2; g) Mn L <sub>2,3</sub>-edge and h) V L <sub>2,3</sub>-edge XAS spectra of NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To better understand the effect of Zr substitution on the materials, the microstructure of the materials was investigated using SEM and TEM. The SEM images (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0003">3a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) show that all the samples are composed of primary particles of nanostructured and these particles are dispersed in the carbon material. The particle size of the NKCMVP-Zr0.2 was significantly smaller than that of the NKCMVP, However, when the substitution of Zr was 0.3 and 0.5, the primary particles showed an obvious particle bonding phenomenon. The SEM mapping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) of the NKCMVP-Zr0.2 was analyzed and the presence of Zr was successfully detected in addition to the Na, K, Ca, Mn, V, P, O, and C elements. The elements were uniformly distributed in the material. Further TEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0003">3b,c</a>) of the NKCMVP-Zr0.2 show that the NKCMVP-Zr0.2 is composed of primary particles with an average size of 200–300 nm (the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0003">3b</a>), and there are clear boundaries between the particles, which are well dispersed within the carbon network, and the crosslinked carbon network constructed by GO constructs a good conductive network structure. From the HR-TEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0003">3d</a>) of the NKCMVP-Zr0.2, it can be clearly observed that the surface of the particles is covered with a continuous and uniform carbon layer with a thickness of ≈10 nm, and the uniform carbon layer not only improves the electronic conductivity on the surface of the material but also effectively inhibits the erosion of the electrolyte on the material bulk to improve the cycling stability of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0039_R_d19537802e1162" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nat. Commun.">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0040_R_d19537802e1162" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0003">3d</a> shows that the bulk structure of the NKCMVP-Zr0.2 has clear lattice fringes, indicating that the synthesized material has good crystallinity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0011_R_d19537802e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Small Struct.">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0021_R_d19537802e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To identify the crystal structure of the material more clearly, the HR-TEM image of the NKCMVP-Zr0.2 was inverse Fourier transformed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0003">3e,f</a>), and measurements were made to obtain an interlayer spacing of ≈0.64 nm for the sample, which corresponds to the (012) crystallographic plane of the Rhombohedral phase. The electron diffraction image of the NKCMVP-Zr0.2 along the [-22-1] direction was further analyzed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0003">3g</a>), and the interplanar spacings of the (110) and (012) planes are ≈0.44 and ≈0.63 nm, respectively, which are in good agreement with the atomic arrangement of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase (the R-3c space group). The TEM EDS images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0003">3h</a>) further demonstrate the presence of Na, K, Ca, Mn, V, Zr, P, O, and C elements, and the mapping images show that each element is uniformly distributed in the bulk phase.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418642-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0e4704b6-cde0-4617-9092-101901660c8c/adfm202418642-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0e4704b6-cde0-4617-9092-101901660c8c/adfm202418642-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1565f673-d19a-4aad-ad87-828b31cf9858/adfm202418642-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0e4704b6-cde0-4617-9092-101901660c8c/adfm202418642-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418642-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The micromorphology of NKCMVP-Zr0.2. a) SEM image; b) TEM image (The inset shows the particle size distribution); c) the partially enlarged TEM image corresponding to b); d) HRTEM image; e) FFT images; f) lattice fringe image; g) SAED image at the [-2, 2, -1] zone axis, and TEM elemental mapping images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate the impact of Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution on regulating the local coordination environment of TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and altering sodium storage behaviors of NMVP, the as-prepared various NMVP samples with different Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution contents were evaluated via galvanostatic charge and discharge tests. As shown in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0004">4a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the discharge specific capacities for NMVP and NKCMVP are 98.1 and 92.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which correspond to initial Coulombic efficiencies (ICEs)&nbsp;of 94.6%, and 96.1%. It is reported that pure NMVP cathode material will cause an irreversible capacity loss due to the JT distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the first cycle, which leads to a decrease of Coulombic efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0008_R_d19537802e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0021_R_d19537802e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After introducing K<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions at Na sites of NMVP, the ICE was increased to 96.1%, revealing that Na-site regulation can efficiently boost Na<sup>+</sup> migration. To further examine the effect of Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution into Mn sites on modulating Na<sup>+</sup> activity in NMVP, the ICEs and first charge-discharge profiles of all samples were analyzed, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0004">4a,b</a>. With increasing Zr<sup>4+</sup> content, the samples show gradual increased ICEs from 96.1% (NKCMVP) to 97.9% (NKCMVP-Zr0.05), 98.0% (NKCMVP-Zr0.1), and 98.3% (NKCMVP-Zr0.2). Such finding shows that TM-site modulation by Zr<sup>4+</sup> can improve Na<sup>+</sup> activity as well. When further raising Zr<sup>4+</sup> amount, the ICEs of NKCMVP-Zr0.3 and NKCMVP-Zr0.5 were reduced to 97.0% and 92.7%, respectively. This implies that too many Zr<sup>4+</sup> ions probably inhibit Na<sup>+</sup> migration in the NMVP. These results reveal that synergistic modulation of local coordination environment of TM and Na sites is favorable to boost Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in the solid.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418642-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/47901797-f088-47e5-b4a0-a69acd478470/adfm202418642-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/47901797-f088-47e5-b4a0-a69acd478470/adfm202418642-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fdcb3760-483c-4bca-a447-6b15c331b345/adfm202418642-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/47901797-f088-47e5-b4a0-a69acd478470/adfm202418642-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418642-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of NKCMVP, NKCMVP-Zr0.05, NKCMVP-Zr0.1, NKCMVP-Zr0.2, NKCMVP-Zr0.3, and NKCMVP-Zr0.5. a) Initial Coulombic efficiencies; b) first charge-discharge profiles at 25&nbsp;°C; c) dQm/dV curves; d) GITT curves and diffusion coefficient of sodium ions of NKCMVP; e) GITT curves and diffusion coefficient of sodium ions of NKCMVP-Zr0.2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the effect of Zr<sup>4+</sup> modulation on specific capacity and working voltage, the as-prepared various samples with different Zr<sup>4+</sup> amounts were employed as cathodes for SIBs. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0004">4b</a>, the initial discharge specific capacities of NKCMVP, NKCMVP-Zr0.05, NKCMVP-Zr0.1, NKCMVP-Zr0.2, NKCMVP-Zr0.3, and NKCMVP-Zr0.5 samples are 92.8, 86.7, 82.3, 82.0, 78.2, and 67.4 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (1 C = 110 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). With increasing the amount of Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution, the products deliver a gradually decreased specific capacity, which is attributed to the reduced electrochemical active Mn caused by introducing electrochemically inactive Zr<sup>4+</sup>. Interestingly, the introduction of Zr<sup>4+</sup> into TM sites obviously alters voltage plateaus of NMVP. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0004">4b</a>, NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.05 exhibit two voltage plateaus at 3.43 and 3.62 V, which is related to the redox couples of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup>, respectively. On further increasing Zr<sup>4+</sup> content, two voltage plateaus gradually merge into one plateau. The similar phenomena were obviously observed as well in the corresponding dQm/dV curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0004">4c</a>. NKCMVP delivers two couples of dQm/dV peaks at 3.43/3.39 and 3.63/3.55 V. On introducing Zr<sup>4+</sup> into Mn sites, the redox peak of NKCMVP-Zr0.05 at 3.6 V gradually shifts to a lower voltage while the redox peak at 3.43 V slightly shifts toward a higher voltage. With further increasing Zr<sup>4+</sup> content, the redox peak of NKCMVP-Zr0.1 at 3.58 V continuously shifts to a lower value while the redox peak at 3.44 V gradually becomes broad. Interestingly, NKCMVP-Zr<sub>x</sub> (x ≥ 0.2) shows one pair of redox peaks, revealing the occurrence of one-step de-sodiaiton/sodiation. The NKCMVP-Zr0.2 has a high oxidation potential ≈3.51 V, which is located roughly at the average of the two oxidation peaks (3.43 and 3.62 V) of the NKCMVP. The merger of the two V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup> plateaus is probably attributed to the regulation of local electron structures of VO<sub>6</sub>/MnO<sub>6</sub> by introducing Zr<sup>4+</sup> with electronegativity (χ&nbsp;&nbsp;= 1.61). It is well known that the redox potential of electrodes is mainly determined by the energy gap between the energy level of electrons participating in redox reactions, and the energy level of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>0</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0041_R_d19537802e1354" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nano Energy">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the redox reactions of Mn<sup>2+</sup> (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aef3cccd-4755-4d92-b4bc-617a871cd660/adfm202418642-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418642:adfm202418642-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418642-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>3</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>2</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^3e_g^2$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>, χ&nbsp;&nbsp;= 1.263) and V<sup>3+</sup> (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c067dee7-30aa-481c-b170-6676cc4584df/adfm202418642-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418642:adfm202418642-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418642-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>2</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>0</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^2e_g^0$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>, χ&nbsp;&nbsp;= 1.545), the <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> d-orbital electron of Mn<sup>2+</sup> at a higher energy state will be usually lost to generate Mn<sup>3+</sup> (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/acc10767-faa1-45a8-87c9-812294543431/adfm202418642-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418642:adfm202418642-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418642-math-0003.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>3</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>1</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^3e_g^1$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>, χ&nbsp;&nbsp;= 1.55) while the <i>t</i><sub>2<i>g</i></sub> d-orbital electron of V<sup>3+</sup> at a low energy state will be lost to generate V<sup>4+</sup>(<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/50f72072-ba7d-4fb4-bf37-7ff6fc7e0a4d/adfm202418642-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418642:adfm202418642-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418642-math-0004.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>1</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>0</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^1e_g^0$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>, χ&nbsp;&nbsp;= 1.795). By introducing Zr<sup>4+</sup> (χ&nbsp;&nbsp;= 1.61), the <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> d-orbital electron of Mn<sup>2+</sup> with lower electronegativity would be more easily lost and results in the reduced energy gap between the energy level of <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> electron and energy level of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na0, thus leading to the reduction of redox potential of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup>. In contrast, V<sup>4+</sup> (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8734080e-3b40-4225-a788-d1cfa9d2ca59/adfm202418642-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202418642:adfm202418642-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202418642-math-0005.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>1</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>0</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^1e_g^0$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>, χ&nbsp;&nbsp;= 1.795) possesses higher electronegativity than that of Zr<sup>4+</sup> (χ&nbsp;&nbsp;= 1.61) and would inhibit the loss of the <i>t</i><sub>2<i>g</i></sub> electron, consequently resulting in the increased energy gap between the energy level of <i>t</i><sub>2<i>g</i></sub> d-orbital electron and the energy level of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>0</sup>. This would induce the increase of redox potential of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>. The similar works have been also demonstrated in Na<sub>2</sub>MnPO<sub>4</sub>F and Li-rich layered cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0041_R_d19537802e1510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nano Energy">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0042_R_d19537802e1510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further demonstrate the effect of Zr<sup>4+</sup> on regulating Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests within a voltage window of 2.5–3.8 V were also employed for the obtained various samples. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0004">4d</a>, the GITT curves of NKCMVP obviously show two voltage plateaus. In addition, the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) profiles of NKCMVP are relatively flat in the first de-sodiation process and show V-shaped like curves in the second de-sodiation process, indicating that the second step Na<sup>+</sup> extraction reaction is more difficult than that in the first step Na<sup>+</sup> counterpart. Based on Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">1</a>) (Supporting Information), the Na<sup>+</sup> chemical diffusion coefficients of all samples are displayed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). After introducing Zr<sup>4+</sup> at Mn sites, the larger Zr<sup>4+</sup> (ionic radius: 0.72 Å) can broaden Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion channel and boost sodium ion migration compared to Mn<sup>2+</sup> (ionic radius: 0.67 Å). The calculated D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> value (≈8.85&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) in the second de-sodiation for NKCMVP is lower one order of magnitude than that (≈5.03×10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) in the first de-sodiation. Similar phenomena were also observed in the corresponding discharge process. After introducing a little mount Zr<sup>4+</sup> into Mn site (<i>x</i> = 0.05, 0.1), the as-prepared NKCMVP-Zr0.05 and NKCMVP-Zr0.1 display similar GITT curves to NKCMVP without substantial improvement in the sodium ion coefficient (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a,b</a>, Supporting Information). When the Zr<sup>4+</sup> content is increased to 0.2, the GITT curves of NKCMVP-Zr0.2 only exhibit a voltage plateau in the both charge and discharge processes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0004">4e</a>). Furthermore, the calculated D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values in the charge and discharge processes show almost the same level of sodium ion diffusion coefficient of ≈1.01 × 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which may be attributed to broadened Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion channels caused by Zr<sup>4+</sup> ions. When the Zr<sup>4+</sup> content is further enhanced to 0.3 and 0.5, a one-step de-sodiation reaction for NMVP can be achieved as well (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c,d</a>, Supporting Information), but their Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficients are lower than that of NKCMVP-Zr0.2, further confirming that excessive Zr-substitution will impede the Na<sup>+</sup> migration. Such results fully confirm that moderate Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution at Mn sites can efficiently alter the local coordination environment of VO<sub>6</sub>/MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and enable the realization of one-step de-sodiation/sodiation in NMVP.</p>
         
         <p>To further examine the impact of Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution at TM sites on regulating Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics, the rate capabilities of the obtained various samples including NKCMVP, NKCMVP-Zr0.05, NKCMVP-Zr0.1, NKCMVP-Zr0.2, NKCMVP-Zr0.3, and NKCMVP-Zr0.5 were investigated at different current rates. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0005">5a</a>, NKCMVP-Zrx shows gradually decreased specific capacities with increasing Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution amount at 0.5 C. On increasing current rates, NKCMVP underwent a gradually reduced capacity deduction from 87.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (0.5 C) to 83.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (1 C), 80.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (2 C), 73.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (5 C), 66.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (10 C), and 50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (15 C). In contrast, several NKCMVP cathodes with Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution show relatively stable specific capacities. Especially for NKCMVP-Zr0.2, the highest specific capacity of 72.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained at 15 C. When the current rate was back to 0.5 C, all samples can well return to the original capacity, indicating excellent Na<sup>+</sup> de-sodiation and sodiation reversibility. More importantly, NKCMVP-Zr0.2 suffers from the smallest capacity decay with increasing current rates among all samples, implying that the moderate Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution at Mn sites can not only boost Na<sup>+</sup> migration but also offer robust lattice structure for enduring repeated Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion reaction. This is also confirmed by the Ragone plots, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0005">5b</a>. NKCMVP-Zr0.2 delivers the most stable energy density retentions from 278.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (192.7 W kg<sup>−1</sup>) to 265.1 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (5998.7 W kg<sup>−1</sup>). In contrast, NKCMVP shows fast attenuation in energy density from 310.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (192.7 W kg<sup>−1</sup>) to 186.8 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (6065.1W kg<sup>−1</sup>). Even at a high power density of ≈ 6000 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, NKCMVP-Zr0.2 still exhibits an energy density of 265.1Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than those of NKCMVP (186.8 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), NKCMVP-Zr0.05 (175.3 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), NKCMVP-Zr0.1 (188.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), NKCMVP-Zr0.3 (245.6 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), and NKCMVP-Zr0.5 (188.8Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>). Compared to NKCMVP, the energy density of NKCMVP-Zr0.2 is increased by 40%. Moreover, NKCMVP-Zr0.2 delivers smaller electrochemical polarization at different current rates than NKCMVP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that appropriate Zr<sup>4+</sup> substitution can boost Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics. Besides superior rate capability, long-term cycling stability is also an important factor for practical application of SIBs. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0005">5c</a>, the obtained various cathode materials were evaluated by galvanostatic charge and discharge cycling tests. After 1000 cycles at 1 C, the capacity retentions of NKCMVP, NKCMVP-Zr0.05, NKCMVP-Zr0.1, NKCMVP-Zr0.2, NKCMVP-Zr0.3, and NKCMVP-Zr0.5 were 75.8%, 87.6%, 91.8%, 94.8%, 97.6%, and 90.8%, respectively. Evat at 5 C, NKCMVP-Zr0.1, NKCMVP-Zr0.2, and NKCMVP-Zr0.3 still exhibit capacity retentions of 93.1%, 97.0%, and 96.1% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), respectively, much higher than that of NKCMVP (73.2%). Such findings reveal that the introduction of Zr<sup>4+</sup> into Mn sites can efficiently improve electrochemical stability of NMVP. To further explore long-term cycling life at a higher current rate, NKCMVP-Zr0.2 was evaluated as well at 10 C. Such cathode still exhibits a reversible capacity of 69.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 92.7% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0005">5d</a>). Furthermore, even at elevated temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0005">5e</a>), a capacity retention (72.6%) of NKCMVP-Zr0.2 was increased by 81.5% compared to NKCMVP (capacity retention: 40.0%) after 1000 cycles at 1 C (50&nbsp;°C). These results fully demonstrate that the substitution of Zr<sup>4+</sup> ions at Mn sites efficiently inhibits the JT distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and simultaneously enhances lattice stability upon repeated de-sodiation/sodiation reaction. In addition, the obtained NKCMVP-Zr0.2 exhibits better cycling stability than those of most of the recently reported NMVP (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). To probe the effect of Zr<sup>4+</sup> on the charge and discharge plateaus upon cycling, the charge and discharge profiles of NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2 were compared, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). After 100 cycles, NKCMVP does not exhibit two voltage plateaus and gradually delivers a slope plateau. In contrast, NKCMVP-Zr0.2 still shows an evident voltage plateau at 3.5 V even after 600 cycles with smaller electrochemical polarization compared to NKCMVP. Thus, the above results further corroborate that the robust Zr-O bonds can provide robust and flexible the coordination environment of TMO<sub>6</sub> for ensuring highly reversible de-sodiation/sodiation reaction.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418642-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/23fdd638-fe0c-4f64-90bd-11731c5b8611/adfm202418642-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/23fdd638-fe0c-4f64-90bd-11731c5b8611/adfm202418642-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b19dfd47-de18-445f-80f5-add2a4d1c261/adfm202418642-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/23fdd638-fe0c-4f64-90bd-11731c5b8611/adfm202418642-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418642-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of NKCMVP, NKCMVP-Zr0.05, NKCMVP-Zr0.1, NKCMVP-Zr0.2, NKCMVP-Zr0.3, and NKCMVP-Zr0.5. a) Rate performances; b) Ragone plots; c) long-term cyclability at 1C; d) long-term cyclability at 10C; e) long-term cyclability at 50&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The sodium storage mechanism and structural evolution of the NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2 electrodes were investigated using ex situ XRD. As can be seen from <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0006">6a</a>, the (110), (113), (116), and (300) peaks located in the range of 19–36° are obviously shifted to a higher angle after charging to 3.8 V. Such finding shows that the NKCMVP has a single-phase reaction mechanism below 3.6 V and a two-phase reaction mechanism from 3.6 to 3.8 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0033_R_d19537802e1754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano">33</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0035_R_d19537802e1754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the XRD patterns of NKCMVP-Zr0.2 only exhibit a slight peak shift at 3.8 V without the presence of new peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0006">6b</a>), suggesting the achievement of a single-phase reaction mechanism during the whole de-sodiation reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0029_R_d19537802e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When discharged to 2.5 V, all the diffraction peaks of NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2 electrodes were nearly return to their initial positions, indicating good reversibility in the process of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion. These results that Zr regulation at TM sites of NMVP can overcome the risks arising from phase transition during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion, and realize one-step de-sodiation.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202418642-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f0be8000-6901-4db9-a948-b0ab300b56b5/adfm202418642-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f0be8000-6901-4db9-a948-b0ab300b56b5/adfm202418642-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b706ec9a-90ae-4c03-9130-a0bf4f216adf/adfm202418642-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f0be8000-6901-4db9-a948-b0ab300b56b5/adfm202418642-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202418642-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202418642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ XRD patterns of NKCMVP and b) ex situ XRD patterns NKCMVP-Zr0.2 electrodes at 0.1C during the first cycle. The ex situ XPS spectra of NKCMVP-Zr0.2. c) V 2p spectra, d) Mn 3s spectra, e) Mn 2p spectra, and f) Zr 3d spectra.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, the oxidation states of V, Mn, and Zr in NKCMVP and NKCMVP-Zr0.2 at different states of charge were analyzed using XPS. The ex situ XPS spectra of V 2p for NKCMVP-Zr0.2 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202418642-fig-0006">6c</a>) exhibit the binding energies of V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> are 516.6 and 525.4 eV in the initial state, respectively. Upon charging to 3.4 V, the V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peak shifted to a higher binding energy (517.6 eV), indicating that V<sup>3+</sup> was oxidized to V<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0015_R_d19537802e1809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0029_R_d19537802e1809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0040_R_d19537802e1809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a>, <a href="#adfm202418642-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202418642-bib-0043_R_d19537802e1809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Funct.
